For a guide to IPA symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">Help:IPA</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Irish language</b> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Gaeilge</i>), also sometimes referred to as <b>Gaelic</b> or the <b>Irish Gaelic language</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Goidelic_languages" title="Goidelic languages">Goidelic language</a> (Gaelic) of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_language_family" class="mw-redirect" title="Indo-European language family">Indo-European language family</a> originating in <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and historically spoken by the <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish people</a>. Irish is spoken as a <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a> by a small minority of Irish people and as a <a href="/wiki/Second_language" title="Second language">second language</a> by a larger group of non-native speakers. \n</p><p>Irish has been the predominant language of the Irish people for most of their recorded history, and they brought it with them to other regions, notably <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Middle_Irish" title="Middle Irish">Middle Irish</a> gave rise to <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx</a> respectively. It has <a href="/wiki/Early_Irish_literature" title="Early Irish literature">the oldest vernacular literature</a> in Western Europe.\n</p><p>Irish has <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Ireland" title="Constitution of Ireland">constitutional</a> status as the <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Ireland" title="Languages of Ireland">national and first official language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a> and is an officially recognised <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Languages of Northern Ireland">minority language in Northern Ireland</a>. It is also among the official <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_European_Union" title="Languages of the European Union">languages of the European Union</a>. The public body <a href="/wiki/Foras_na_Gaeilge" title="Foras na Gaeilge">Foras na Gaeilge</a> is responsible for the promotion of the language throughout the island of Ireland.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Dialects"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Dialects</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Munster"><span aspect</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#An_Caighde\xc3\xa1n_Oifigi\xc3\xbail"><span class="tocnumber">6.6</span> <span class="toctext">An Caighde\xc3\xa1n Oifigi\xc3\xbail</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Phonology"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Phonology</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Syntax_and_morphology"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Syntax and morphology</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Initial_mutations"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Initial mutations</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Orthography"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Orthography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">10.1</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Literature"><span class="tocnumber">12.1</span> <span class="toctext">Literature</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Grammar_and_pronunciation"><span class="tocnumber">12.2</span> <span class="toctext">Grammar and pronunciation</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Dictionaries"><span class="tocnumber">12.3</span> <span class="toctext">Dictionaries</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text"><a href="#An_Caighde\xc3\xa1n_Oifigi\xc3\xbail">An Caighde\xc3\xa1n Oifigi\xc3\xbail</a></i> (the official written standard) the name of the language is <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Gaeilge</i> (<small>Irish pronunciation:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Irish" title="Help:IPA/Irish">[\xcb\x88\xc9\xa1e\xcb\x90l\xca\xb2\xc9\x9f\xc9\x99]</a></span>). Before the spelling reform of 1948, this form was spelled <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Gaedhilge</i>; originally this was the <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive</a> of <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Gaedhealg</i>, the form used in <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Irish_language#Early_Modern_Irish" title="History of the Irish language">Classical Irish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Older spellings of this include <i lang="mga" title="Middle Irish language text">Gaoidhealg</i> in Classical Irish <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88\xc9\xa1e\xcb\x90\xca\x9d\xc9\x99l\xcb\xa0\xc9\xa1]</span> and <i lang="sga" title="Old Irish language text">Go\xc3\xaddelc</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88\xc9\xa1oi\xc3\xb0el\xcb\xa0\xc9\xa1]</span> in <a href="/wiki/Old_Irish" title="Old Irish">Old Irish</a>. The modern spelling results from the deletion of the silent <i>dh</i> in the middle of <i>Gaedhilge</i>, whereas <a href="/wiki/Goidelic" class="mw-redirect" title="Goidelic">Goidelic</a>, used to refer to the language family including Irish, is derived from the Old Irish term.\n</p><p>Other forms of the name found in the various modern Irish dialects (in addition to south Connacht <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Gaeilge</i> above) include <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Gaedhilic/Gaeilic/Gaeilig</i> (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88\xc9\xa1e\xcb\x90l\xca\xb2\xc9\xaac]</span>) or <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Gaedhlag</i> (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88\xc9\xa1e\xcb\x90l\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0\xc9\x99\xc9\xa1]</span>) in <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Irish" title="Ulster Irish">Ulster Irish</a> and northern Connacht Irish and <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Gaedhealaing/Gaoluinn/Gaelainn</i> (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xcb\x88\xc9\xa1e\xcb\x90l\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0\xc9\xaa\xc5\x8b\xca\xb2/\xcb\x88\xc9\xa1e\xcb\x90l\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0\xc9\xaan\xca\xb2]</span>)<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Munster_Irish" title="Munster Irish">Munster Irish</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/English_language_in_Europe" title="English language in Europe">In Europe</a><a href="/wiki/English_language_in_Asia" class="mw-redirect" title="English language in Asia"> and in Asia</a> the language is usually referred to as <i>Irish</i>, with <i>Gaelic</i> or <i>Irish Gaelic</i> used in some instances elsewhere.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The term <i>Irish Gaelic</i> is often used when English speakers discuss the relationship between the three Goidelic languages (Irish, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx</a>).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Irish_language" title="History of the Irish language">History of the Irish language</a></div>\n<p>Written Irish is first attested in <a href="/wiki/Ogham" title="Ogham">Ogham</a> inscriptions from the 4th century AD, a stage of the language known as <a href="/wiki/Primitive_Irish" title="Primitive Irish">Primitive Irish</a>. These writings have been found throughout Ireland and the west coast of Great Britain. Primitive Irish transitioned into <a href="/wiki/Old_Irish" title="Old Irish">Old Irish</a> through the 5th century. Old Irish, dating from the 6th century, used the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> and is attested primarily in <a href="/wiki/Marginalia" title="Marginalia">marginalia</a> to Latin manuscripts. During this time, the Irish language absorbed some <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> words, some via <a href="/wiki/Old_Welsh" title="Old Welsh">Old Welsh</a>, including ecclesiastical terms: examples are <i>easpag</i> (bishop) from <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">episcopus</span>, and <i>Domhnach</i> (Sunday, from <span class="smallcaps" style="font-variant:small-caps;">dominica</span>).\n</p><p>By the 10th century, Old Irish had evolved into <a href="/wiki/Middle_Irish" title="Middle Irish">Middle Irish</a>, which was spoken throughout Ireland and in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a>. It is the language of a large corpus of literature, including the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Cycle" title="Ulster Cycle">Ulster Cycle</a>. From the 12th century, Middle Irish began to evolve into modern Irish in Ireland, into <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a> in Scotland, and into the <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx language</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a>. Early Modern Irish, dating from the 13th century, was the basis of the literary language of both Ireland and Gaelic-speaking Scotland. Modern Irish, as attested in the work of such writers as <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Keating" title="Geoffrey Keating">Geoffrey Keating</a>, may be said to date from the 17th century, and was the medium of popular literature from that time on.\n</p><p>From the 18th century on, the language lost ground in the east of the country. The reasons behind this shift were complex but came down to a number of factors:\n</p>\n<ol><li>discouragement of its use by Anglo-British administrations</li>\n<li>the Catholic church supporting the use of English over Irish</li>\n<li>the spread of bilingualism from the 1750s, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Language_shift" title="Language shift">language shift</a><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup></li></ol>\n<p>It was a change characterised by <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossia</a> (two languages being used by the same community in different social and economic situations) and transitional bilingualism (monoglot Irish-speaking grandparents with bilingual children and monoglot English-speaking grandchildren). By the mid-18th century, English was becoming a language of the Catholic middle class, the Catholic Church and public intellectuals, especially in the east of the country. Increasingly, as the value of English became apparent, the prohibition on Irish in schools had the sanction of parents.<sup id="cite_ref-\xc3\x93_Gr\xc3\xa1da_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-\xc3\x93_Gr\xc3\xa1da-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Once it became apparent that immigration to the United States and Canada was likely for a large portion of the population, the importance of learning English became relevant. This allowed the new immigrants to get jobs in areas other than farming. It has been estimated that, due to the immigration to the United States because of the Famine, anywhere from a quarter to a third of the immigrants were Irish speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Irish was not marginal to Ireland\'s modernisation in the 19th century, as often assumed. In the first half of the century there were still around three million people for whom Irish was the primary language, and their numbers alone made them a cultural and social force. Irish speakers often insisted on using the language in law courts (even when they knew English), and Irish was also common in commercial transactions. The language was heavily implicated in the "devotional revolution" which marked the standardisation of Catholic religious practice and was also widely used in a political context. Down to the time of the Great Famine and even afterwards, the language was in use by all classes, Irish being an urban as well as a rural language.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>This linguistic dynamism was reflected in the efforts of certain public intellectuals to counter the decline of the language. At the end of the 19th century, they launched the <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_revival" title="Gaelic revival">Gaelic revival</a> in an attempt to encourage the learning and use of Irish, although few adult learners mastered the language.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> The vehicle of the revival was the Gaelic League (<a href="/wiki/Conradh_na_Gaeilge" title="Conradh na Gaeilge">Conradh na Gaeilge</a>), and particular emphasis was placed on the folk tradition, which in Irish is particularly rich. Efforts were also made to develop journalism and a modern literature.\n</p><p>Although it has been noted that the Catholic Church played a role in the decline of the Irish language before the Gaelic Revival, the Protestant Church of Ireland also made only minor efforts to encourage use of Irish in a religious context. An Irish translation of the Old Testament by Leinsterman <a href="/wiki/Muircheartach_%C3%93g_%C3%93_C%C3%ADonga" title="Muircheartach \xc3\x93g \xc3\x93 C\xc3\xadonga">Muircheartach \xc3\x93 C\xc3\xadonga</a>, commissioned by <a href="/wiki/William_Bedell" title="William Bedell">Bishop Bedell</a>, was published after 1685 along with a translation of the New Testament. Otherwise, Anglicisation was seen as synonymous with \'civilising\'" of the native Irish. Currently, modern day Irish speakers in the church are pushing for language revival.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_status">Current status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Current status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Status_of_the_Irish_language" title="Status of the Irish language">Status of the Irish language</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Republic_of_Ireland">Republic of Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Republic of Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Irish is recognised by the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Ireland" title="Constitution of Ireland">Constitution of Ireland</a> as the national and first official language of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Ireland" title="Republic of Ireland">Republic of Ireland</a> (English is the other official language). Despite this, almost all government debates and business are conducted in English.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In 1938, the founder of <a href="/wiki/Conradh_na_Gaeilge" title="Conradh na Gaeilge">Conradh na Gaeilge</a> (Gaelic League), <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Hyde" title="Douglas Hyde">Douglas Hyde</a>, was inaugurated as the first <a href="/wiki/President_of_Ireland" title="President of Ireland">President of Ireland</a>. The record of his delivering his inaugural <i>Declaration of Office</i> in <a href="/wiki/County_Roscommon" title="County Roscommon">Roscommon</a> Irish is one of only a few recordings of that dialect.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a> in 1922 (see also <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="History of the Republic of Ireland">History of the Republic of Ireland</a>), a degree of proficiency in Irish was required of all those newly appointed to the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Service_of_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Civil Service of the Republic of Ireland">Civil Service of the Republic of Ireland</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Postal_worker" title="Postal worker">postal workers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tax_collector" title="Tax collector">tax collectors</a>, agricultural inspectors, <a href="/wiki/Garda_S%C3%ADoch%C3%A1na" title="Garda S\xc3\xadoch\xc3\xa1na">Garda S\xc3\xadoch\xc3\xa1na</a>, etc. By law if a Garda was stopped and addressed in Irish he had to respond in Irish as well.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Proficiency in just one official language for entrance to the public service was introduced in 1974, in part through the actions of protest organisations like the <a href="/wiki/Language_Freedom_Movement" title="Language Freedom Movement">Language Freedom Movement</a>.\n</p><p>Although the Irish requirement was also dropped for wider public service jobs, Irish remains a required subject of study in all schools within the Republic which receive public money (see also <a href="/wiki/Education_in_the_Republic_of_Ireland" title="Education in the Republic of Ireland">Education in the Republic of Ireland</a>). Those wishing to teach in primary schools in the State must also pass a compulsory examination called <i>Scr\xc3\xbad\xc3\xba C\xc3\xa1il\xc3\xadochta sa Ghaeilge</i>. The need for a pass in <a href="/wiki/Leaving_Certificate_(Ireland)" title="Leaving Certificate (Ireland)">Leaving Certificate</a> Irish or English for entry to the <a href="/wiki/Garda_S%C3%ADoch%C3%A1na" title="Garda S\xc3\xadoch\xc3\xa1na">Garda S\xc3\xadoch\xc3\xa1na</a> (police) was introduced in September 2005, and recruits are given lessons in the language during their two years of training. The most important official documents of the Irish government must be published in both Irish and English or Irish alone (in accordance with the Official Languages Act 2003, enforced by <i>An Coimisin\xc3\xa9ir Teanga</i>, the Irish language ombudsman).\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/National_University_of_Ireland" title="National University of Ireland">National University of Ireland</a> requires all students wishing to embark on a degree course in the NUI federal system to pass the subject of Irish in the Leaving Certificate or <a href="/wiki/General_Certificate_of_Education" title="General Certificate of Education">GCE</a>/<a href="/wiki/GCSE" class="mw-redirect" title="GCSE">GCSE</a> examinations.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Exemptions are made from this requirement for students born outside of the Republic of Ireland, those who were born in the Republic but completed primary education outside it, and students diagnosed with <a href="/wiki/Dyslexia" title="Dyslexia">dyslexia</a>. <a href="/wiki/NUI_Galway" title="NUI Galway">NUI Galway</a> is required to appoint people who are competent in the Irish language, as long as they are also competent in all other aspects of the vacancy to which they are appointed. This requirement is laid down by the University College Galway Act, 1929 (Section 3).<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The University faced controversy, however, in 2016 when it was announced that the next president of the University would not have any Irish. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Misneach&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Misneach (page does not exist)">Misneach</a> staged a number of protests against this decision. It was announced in September 2017 that Ciar\xc3\xa1n \xc3\x93 h\xc3\x93gartaigh, a fluent Irish speaker, will be NUIG\'s 13th president.\n</p><p>For a number of years there has been vigorous debate in political, academic and other circles about the failure of most students in mainstream (English-medium) schools to achieve competence in the language, even after fourteen years.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The concomitant decline in the number of traditional native speakers has also been a cause of great concern.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> In 2007, filmmaker <a href="/wiki/Manch%C3%A1n_Magan" title="Manch\xc3\xa1n Magan">Manch\xc3\xa1n Magan</a> found few speakers and some incredulity while speaking only Irish in Dublin. He was unable to accomplish some everyday tasks, as portrayed in his documentary <i><a href="/wiki/No_B%C3%A9arla" title="No B\xc3\xa9arla">No B\xc3\xa9arla</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is, however, a growing body of Irish speakers in urban areas, most coming through the  <i><a href="/wiki/Gaelscoil" title="Gaelscoil">Gaelscoileanna</a></i>, national schools, and a small number of secondary schools, in which Irish is the sole language of instruction. These Irish-medium schools send a much higher<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> proportion of pupils on to third-level education than do "mainstream" schools, and it seems increasingly likely that, within a generation, habitual users of Irish will typically be members of an urban, middle class and highly educated minority.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Parliamentary legislation is supposed to be available in both Irish and English but is frequently only available in English. This is notwithstanding that Article 25.4 of the Constitution of Ireland requires that an "official translation" of any law in one official language be provided immediately in the other official language, if not already passed in both official languages.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 2016, it was reported that many people worldwide were learning Irish through the <a href="/wiki/Duolingo" title="Duolingo">Duolingo</a> app.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>  Irish president <a href="/wiki/Michael_D._Higgins" title="Michael D. Higgins">Michael Higgins</a> officially honoured several volunteer translators for developing the Irish edition, and said the push for Irish language rights remains an "unfinished project".<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 2016 census, around 10% of respondents stated that they spoke Irish, either daily or weekly.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gaeltacht">Gaeltacht</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Gaeltacht">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gaeltacht" title="Gaeltacht">Gaeltacht</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gaeltacht.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Gaeltacht.svg/250px-Gaeltacht.svg.png" width="250" height="321" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Gaeltacht.svg/375px-Gaeltacht.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Gaeltacht.svg/500px-Gaeltacht.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="906" data-file-height="1163" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gaeltacht.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Official <a href="/wiki/Gaeltacht" title="Gaeltacht">Gaeltacht</a> areas</div></div></div>\n<p>There are rural areas of Ireland where Irish is still spoken daily to some extent as a <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a>. These regions are known individually and collectively as the <a href="/wiki/Gaeltacht" title="Gaeltacht">Gaeltacht</a>, or in the plural as <i>Gaeltachta\xc3\xad</i>. While the Gaeltacht\'s fluent Irish speakers, whose numbers have been estimated at twenty or thirty thousand,<sup id="cite_ref-Siggins_5_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siggins_5-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> are a minority of the total number of fluent Irish speakers, they represent a higher concentration of Irish speakers than other parts of the country and it is only in Gaeltacht areas that Irish continues, to some extent, to be spoken as a community vernacular.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Percentage_stating_they_speak_Irish_daily_outside_the_education_system_in_the_2011_census.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Percentage_stating_they_speak_Irish_daily_outside_the_education_system_in_the_2011_census.png/250px-Percentage_stating_they_speak_Irish_daily_outside_the_education_system_in_the_2011_census.png" width="250" height="352" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Percentage_stating_they_speak_Irish_daily_outside_the_education_system_in_the_2011_census.png/375px-Percentage_stating_they_speak_Irish_daily_outside_the_education_system_in_the_2011_census.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Percentage_stating_they_speak_Irish_daily_outside_the_education_system_in_the_2011_census.png/500px-Percentage_stating_they_speak_Irish_daily_outside_the_education_system_in_the_2011_census.png 2x" data-file-width="10560" data-file-height="14885" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Percentage_stating_they_speak_Irish_daily_outside_the_education_system_in_the_2011_census.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The percentage of respondents who said they spoke Irish daily outside the education system in the 2011 census in the State.</div></div></div>\n<p>According to data compiled by the <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Community,_Equality_and_Gaeltacht_Affairs" class="mw-redirect" title="Department of Community, Equality and Gaeltacht Affairs">Department of Community, Equality and Gaeltacht Affairs</a>, only one quarter of households in officially Gaeltacht areas are fluent in Irish. The author of a detailed analysis of the survey, Donncha \xc3\x93 h\xc3\x89allaithe of the <a href="/wiki/Galway-Mayo_Institute_of_Technology" title="Galway-Mayo Institute of Technology">Galway-Mayo Institute of Technology</a>, described the Irish language policy followed by Irish governments as a "complete and absolute disaster". <i><a href="/wiki/The_Irish_Times" title="The Irish Times">The Irish Times</a></i>, referring to his analysis published in the Irish language newspaper <i><a href="/wiki/Foinse" title="Foinse">Foinse</a></i>, quoted him as follows: "It is an absolute indictment of successive Irish Governments that at the foundation of the Irish State there were 250,000 fluent Irish speakers living in Irish-speaking or semi Irish-speaking areas, but the number now is between 20,000 and 30,000".<sup id="cite_ref-Siggins_5_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Siggins_5-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1920s, when the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Free_State" title="Irish Free State">Irish Free State</a> was founded, Irish was still a vernacular in some western coastal areas.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> In the 1930s, areas where more than 25% of the population spoke Irish were classified as Gaeltacht. Today, the strongest Gaeltacht areas, numerically and socially, are those of South <a href="/wiki/Connemara" title="Connemara">Connemara</a>, the west of the <a href="/wiki/Dingle_Peninsula" title="Dingle Peninsula">Dingle Peninsula</a> and northwest Donegal, where many residents still use Irish as their primary language. These areas are often referred to as the <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">F\xc3\xador-Ghaeltacht</i> ("true Gaeltacht"), a term originally officially applied to areas where over 50% of the population spoke Irish.\n</p><p>There are larger Gaeltacht regions in <a href="/wiki/County_Galway" title="County Galway">County Galway</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Contae na Gaillimhe</i>), including Connemara (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Conamara</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Aran_Islands" title="Aran Islands">Aran Islands</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Oile\xc3\xa1in \xc3\x81rann</i>), <a href="/wiki/Carraroe" title="Carraroe">Carraroe</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">An Cheathr\xc3\xba Rua</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Spiddal" title="Spiddal">Spiddal</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">An Spid\xc3\xa9al</i>), on the west coast of <a href="/wiki/County_Donegal" title="County Donegal">County Donegal</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Contae Dh\xc3\xban na nGall</i>), and on the <a href="/wiki/Dingle_Peninsula" title="Dingle Peninsula">Dingle</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Corca Dhuibhne</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Iveragh_Peninsula" title="Iveragh Peninsula">Iveragh Peninsulas</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Uibh Rathach</i>) in <a href="/wiki/County_Kerry" title="County Kerry">County Kerry</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Contae Chiarra\xc3\xad</i>).\n</p><p>Smaller ones also exist in Counties <a href="/wiki/County_Mayo" title="County Mayo">Mayo</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Contae Mhaigh Eo</i>), <a href="/wiki/County_Meath" title="County Meath">Meath</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Contae na M\xc3\xad</i>), <a href="/wiki/County_Waterford" title="County Waterford">Waterford</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">An Rinn, Contae Phort L\xc3\xa1irge</i>), and <a href="/wiki/County_Cork" title="County Cork">Cork</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Contae Chorca\xc3\xad</i>). <a href="/wiki/Gweedore" title="Gweedore">Gweedore</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Gaoth Dobhair</i>), County Donegal, is the largest Gaeltacht parish in Ireland.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:169px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aire-leanai.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e9/Aire-leanai.jpg" width="167" height="206" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="167" data-file-height="206" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aire-leanai.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sign reads, <i>Caution, Children</i></div></div></div>\n<p>Irish language summer colleges in the Gaeltacht are attended by tens of thousands of teenagers annually. Students live with Gaeltacht families, attend classes, participate in sports, go to <i><a href="/wiki/C%C3%A8ilidh" title="C\xc3\xa8ilidh">c\xc3\xa9ilithe</a></i> and are obliged to speak Irish. All aspects of Irish culture and tradition are encouraged.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Ireland">Northern Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Northern Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Irish_language_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Irish language in Northern Ireland">Irish language in Northern Ireland</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Multilingual_sign_Department_Culture_Leisure_Arts_Northern_Ireland.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Multilingual_sign_Department_Culture_Leisure_Arts_Northern_Ireland.jpg/220px-Multilingual_sign_Department_Culture_Leisure_Arts_Northern_Ireland.jpg" width="220" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Multilingual_sign_Department_Culture_Leisure_Arts_Northern_Ireland.jpg/330px-Multilingual_sign_Department_Culture_Leisure_Arts_Northern_Ireland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Multilingual_sign_Department_Culture_Leisure_Arts_Northern_Ireland.jpg/440px-Multilingual_sign_Department_Culture_Leisure_Arts_Northern_Ireland.jpg 2x" data-file-width="962" data-file-height="869" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Multilingual_sign_Department_Culture_Leisure_Arts_Northern_Ireland.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sign for the Department of Culture, Arts and Leisure in Northern Ireland, in English, Irish and <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Scots_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulster Scots language">Ulster Scots</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Before the partition of Ireland in 1921, Irish was recognised as a school subject and as "Celtic" in some third level institutions. Between 1921 and 1972, <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> had devolved government. During those years the political party holding power in the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Parliament of Northern Ireland">Stormont Parliament</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Unionist_Party" title="Ulster Unionist Party">Ulster Unionist Party</a> (UUP), was hostile to the language.  The context of this hostility was the use of the language by nationalists.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In broadcasting, there was an exclusion on the reporting of minority cultural issues, and Irish was excluded from radio and television for almost the first fifty years of the previous devolved government.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The language received a degree of <a href="/wiki/Irish_language_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Irish language in Northern Ireland">formal recognition in Northern Ireland</a> from the United Kingdom, under the 1998 <a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">Good Friday Agreement</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-belfast_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-belfast-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> and then, in 2003, by the British government\'s ratification in respect of the language of the <a href="/wiki/European_Charter_for_Regional_or_Minority_Languages" title="European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages">European Charter for Regional or Minority Languages</a>. In the 2006 <a href="/wiki/St_Andrews_Agreement" title="St Andrews Agreement">St Andrews Agreement</a> the British government promised to enact "an Irish Language Act" to promote the language<sup id="cite_ref-bbc13dec2006_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc13dec2006-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> but as of 2014 it has yet to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\nThe Irish language has often been used as a bargaining chip during government formation in Northern Ireland, prompting protests from organisations and groups such as <a href="/w/index.php?title=An_Dream_Dearg&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="An Dream Dearg (page does not exist)">An Dream Dearg</a>. There is currently an ongoing debate in relation to the status of the language in the form of an Irish Language Act. An Dream Dearg have launched a campaign in favour of such an Act called <i>Acht na Gaeilge Anois</i> ("Irish Language Act Now"). <sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Parliament">European Parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: European Parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Irish became an official language of the EU on 1 January 2007, meaning that MEPs with Irish fluency can now speak the language in the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament" title="European Parliament">European Parliament</a> and at committees, although in the case of the latter they have to give prior notice to a simultaneous interpreter in order to ensure that what they say can be interpreted into other languages. While an official <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_European_Union" title="Languages of the European Union">language of the European Union</a>, only co-decision regulations must be available in Irish for the moment, due to a renewable five-year derogation on what has to be translated, requested by the Irish Government when negotiating the language\'s new official status. Any expansion in the range of documents to be translated will depend on the results of the first five-year review and on whether the Irish authorities decide to seek an extension. The Irish government has committed itself to train the necessary number of translators and interpreters and to bear the related costs.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Derogation is expected to end completely by 2022.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Before Irish became an official language it was afforded the status of treaty language and only the highest-level documents of the EU were made available in Irish.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outside_Ireland">Outside Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Outside Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Irish_language_outside_Ireland" title="Irish language outside Ireland">Irish language outside Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irish_language_in_Newfoundland" title="Irish language in Newfoundland">Irish language in Newfoundland</a></div>\n<p>The Irish language was carried abroad in the modern period by a vast <a href="/wiki/Diaspora" title="Diaspora">diaspora</a>, chiefly to <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a> and North America, but also to <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>.\nThe first large movements began in the 17th century, largely as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Cromwellian_conquest_of_Ireland" title="Cromwellian conquest of Ireland">Cromwellian conquest of Ireland</a>, which saw many Irish sent to the <a href="/wiki/West_Indies" title="West Indies">West Indies</a>. Irish emigration to the United States was well established by the 18th century, and was reinforced in the 1840s by thousands fleeing from the Famine. This flight also affected Britain. Up until that time most emigrants spoke Irish as their first language, though English was establishing itself as the primary language. Irish speakers had first arrived in Australia in the late 18th century as convicts and soldiers, and many Irish-speaking settlers followed, particularly in the 1860s. New Zealand also received some of this influx. Argentina was the only non-English-speaking country to receive large numbers of Irish emigrants, and there were few Irish speakers among them.\n</p><p>Relatively few of the emigrants were literate in Irish, but manuscripts in the language were brought to both Australia and the United States, and it was in the United States that the first newspaper to make significant use of Irish was established: <a href="/wiki/An_Gael" title="An Gael">An Gaodhal</a>. In Australia, too, the language found its way into print. The Gaelic revival, which started in Ireland in the 1890s, found a response abroad, with branches of <a href="/wiki/Conradh_na_Gaeilge" title="Conradh na Gaeilge">Conradh na Gaeilge</a> being established in all the countries to which Irish speakers had emigrated.\n</p><p>The decline of Irish in Ireland and a slowing of emigration helped to ensure a decline in the language abroad, along with natural attrition in the host countries. Despite this, small groups of enthusiasts continued to learn and cultivate Irish in diaspora countries and elsewhere, a trend which strengthened in the second half of the 20th century. Today the language is taught at tertiary level in North America, Australia and Europe, and Irish speakers outside Ireland contribute to journalism and literature in the language. There are significant Irish-speaking networks in the United States and Canada;<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> figures released for the period 2006\xe2\x80\x932008 show that 22,279 Americans claimed to speak Irish at home.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Irish language is also one of the languages of the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_League" title="Celtic League">Celtic League</a>, a non-governmental organisation that promotes self-determination and Celtic identity and culture in Ireland, Scotland, Wales, <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a> and the Isle of Man, known as the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_nations" title="Celtic nations">Celtic nations</a>. It places particular emphasis on the indigenous Celtic languages. It is recognised by the United Nations as a non-governmental organisation with "Roster Status" and is part of the UN\'s Economic and Social Council. The organisation has branches in all the Celtic nations and in <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonia</a>, Argentina, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, US, and <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, UK.\n</p><p>Irish was spoken as a community language until the early 20th century on the island of <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a>, in a form known as <a href="/wiki/Irish_language_in_Newfoundland" title="Irish language in Newfoundland">Newfoundland Irish</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use">Use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2><p>\nThe following 2016 census data shows:</p><blockquote><p>The total number of people who answered \xe2\x80\x98yes\xe2\x80\x99 to being able to speak Irish in April 2016 was 1,761,420, a slight decrease (0.7 per cent) on the 2011 figure of 1,774,437. This represents 39.8 per cent of respondents compared with 41.4 in 2011... Of the 73,803 daily Irish speakers (outside the education system), 20,586 (27.9%) lived in Gaeltacht areas.</p></blockquote>\n<h2><span id="Daily_Irish_Speakers_in_Gaeltacht_area.2C_2011-2016"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Daily_Irish_Speakers_in_Gaeltacht_area,_2011-2016">Daily Irish Speakers in Gaeltacht area, 2011-2016</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Daily Irish Speakers in Gaeltacht area, 2011-2016">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th width="120px">Gaeltacht Area\n</th>\n<th
