align="center"><i>All Gaeltacht Areas</i>\n</td>\n<td align="right"><i>23,175</i>\n</td>\n<td align="right"><i>20,586</i>\n</td>\n<td align="right"><i>-2,589</i>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<blockquote><p><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">Dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Dialects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Irish is represented by several traditional <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialects</a> and by various varieties of "urban" Irish. The latter have acquired lives of their own and a growing number of native speakers. Differences between the dialects make themselves felt in stress, intonation, vocabulary and structural features.\n</p><p>Roughly speaking, the three major dialect areas which survive coincide roughly with the provinces of <a href="/wiki/Munster" title="Munster">Munster</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">C\xc3\xbaige Mumhan</i>), <a href="/wiki/Connacht" title="Connacht">Connacht</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">C\xc3\xbaige Chonnacht</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">C\xc3\xbaige Uladh</i>). Records of some dialects of <a href="/wiki/Leinster" title="Leinster">Leinster</a> (<i>C\xc3\xbaige Laighean</i>) were made by the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Folklore_Commission" title="Irish Folklore Commission">Irish Folklore Commission</a> and others<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>. <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a>, in eastern Canada, had a form of Irish derived from the Munster Irish of the later 18th century (see <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_Irish" class="mw-redirect" title="Newfoundland Irish">Newfoundland Irish</a>).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Munster">Munster</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Munster">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Munster_Irish" title="Munster Irish">Munster Irish</a></div>\n<p>Munster Irish is the dialect spoken in the Gaeltacht areas of the three counties <a href="/wiki/County_Cork" title="County Cork">Cork</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Contae Chorca\xc3\xad</i>), <a href="/wiki/County_Kerry" title="County Kerry">Kerry</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Contae Chiarra\xc3\xad</i>), <a href="/wiki/County_Waterford" title="County Waterford">Waterford</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Contae Phort L\xc3\xa1irge</i>).  The Gaeltacht areas of Cork can be found in <a href="/wiki/Cape_Clear_Island" title="Cape Clear Island">Cape Clear Island</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Oile\xc3\xa1n Chl\xc3\xa9ire</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Muskerry" title="Muskerry">Muskerry</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">M\xc3\xbascra\xc3\xad</i>); those of Kerry lie in <a href="/wiki/Corca_Dhuibhne" class="mw-redirect" title="Corca Dhuibhne">Corca Dhuibhne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iveragh_Peninsula" title="Iveragh Peninsula">Iveragh Peninsula</a>; and those of Waterford in <a href="/wiki/Ring,_County_Waterford" title="Ring, County Waterford">Ring</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">An Rinn</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Old_Parish" title="Old Parish">Old Parish</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">An Sean Phobal</i>), both of which together form <a href="/wiki/Gaeltacht_na_nD%C3%A9ise" title="Gaeltacht na nD\xc3\xa9ise">Gaeltacht na nD\xc3\xa9ise</a>.  Of the three counties, the Irish spoken in Cork and Kerry are quiet similar while that of Waterford is more distinct.\n</p><p>Some typical features of Munster Irish are:\n</p>\n<ol><li>The use of endings to show person on verbs in parallel with a pronominal subject system, thus "I must" is in Munster <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">caithfead</i> as well as <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">caithfidh m\xc3\xa9</i>, while other dialects prefer <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">caithfidh m\xc3\xa9</i> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">m\xc3\xa9</i> means "I"). "I was and you were" is <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Bh\xc3\xados agus bh\xc3\xads</i> as well as <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Bh\xc3\xad m\xc3\xa9 agus bh\xc3\xad t\xc3\xba</i> in Munster, but more commonly <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Bh\xc3\xad m\xc3\xa9 agus bh\xc3\xad t\xc3\xba</i> in other dialects. Note that these are strong tendencies, and the personal forms <i>Bh\xc3\xados</i> etc. are used in the West and North, particularly when the words are last in the clause.</li>\n<li>Use of <a href="/wiki/Dependent_and_independent_verb_forms" title="Dependent and independent verb forms">independent/dependent forms of verbs</a> that are not included in the Standard. For example, "I see" in Munster is <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">ch\xc3\xadm</i>, which is the independent form \xe2\x80\x93 Northern Irish also uses a similar form, <i>tch\xc3\xadm</i>), whereas "I do not see" is <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">n\xc3\xad fheicim</i>, <i>feicim</i> being the dependent form, which is used after particles such as <i>n\xc3\xad</i> "not"). <i>Ch\xc3\xadm</i> is replaced by <i>feicim</i> in the Standard. Similarly, the traditional form preserved in Munster <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">bheirim</i> I give/<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">n\xc3\xad thugaim</i> is <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">tugaim</i>/<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">n\xc3\xad thugaim</i> in the Standard; <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">gheibhim</i> I get/<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">n\xc3\xad bhfaighim</i> is <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">faighim</i>/<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">n\xc3\xad bhfaighim</i>.</li>\n<li>When before -<i>nn</i>, -<i>m</i>, -<i>rr</i>, -<i>rd</i>, -<i>ll</i> and so on, in monosyllabic words and in the stressed syllable of multisyllabic words where the syllable is followed by a consonant, some short vowels are lengthened while others are <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthongised</a>, thus <i>ceann</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[caun]</span> "head", <i>cam</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[k\xc9\x91um]</span> "crooked", <i>gearr</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\x9fa\xcb\x90r]</span> "short", <i>ord</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[o\xcb\x90rd]</span> "sledgehammer", <i>gall</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xc9\xa1\xc9\x91ul]</span> "foreigner, non-Gael", <i>iontas</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[u\xcb\x90nt\xc9\x99s]</span> "a wonder, a marvel", <i>comp\xc3\xa1nach</i> <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[k\xc9\x99um\xcb\x88p\xc9\x91\xcb\x90n\xc9\x99x]</span> "companion, mate", etc.</li>\n<li>A <a href="/wiki/Copula_(linguistics)" title="Copula (linguistics)">copular</a> construction involving <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">ea</i> "it" is frequently used. Thus "I am an Irish person" can be said <i>is \xc3\x89ireannach m\xc3\xa9</i> and <i>\xc3\x89ireannach is ea m\xc3\xa9</i> in Munster; there is a subtle difference in meaning, however, the first choice being a simple statement of fact, while the second brings emphasis onto the word <i>\xc3\x89ireannach</i>.  In effect the construction is a type of "<a href="/wiki/Topicalization" title="Topicalization">fronting</a>".</li>\n<li>Both masculine and feminine words are subject to lenition after <i>insan</i> (<i>sa</i>/<i>san</i>) "in the", <i>den</i> "of the" and <i>don</i> "to/for the"&#160;: <i>sa tsiopa</i>, "in the shop", compared to the Standard <i>sa siopa</i> (the Standard lenites only feminine nouns in the dative in these cases).</li>\n<li>Eclipsis of <i>f</i> after <i>sa</i>: <i>sa bhfeirm</i>, "in the farm", instead of <i>san fheirm</i>.</li>\n<li>Eclipsis of <i>t</i> and <i>d</i> after preposition + singular article, with all prepositions except after <i>insan</i>, <i>den</i> and <i>don</i>: <i>ar an dtigh</i> "on the house", <i>ag an ndoras</i> "at the door".</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">Stress</a> falls in general found on the second syllable of a word when the first syllable contains a short vowel, and the second syllable contains a long vowel, diphthong, or is <i>-(e)ach</i>, e.g. <span lang="ga" title="Irish language text">bio<i>r\xc3\xa1n</i></span> ("pin"), as opposed to <span lang="ga" title="Irish language text"><i>bio</i>r\xc3\xa1n</span> in Connacht and Ulster.</li></ol>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Connacht">Connacht</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Connacht">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Connacht_Irish" title="Connacht Irish">Connacht Irish</a></div>\n<p>Historically, Connacht Irish represents the westernmost remnant of a dialect area which once stretched from east to west across the centre of Ireland. The strongest dialect of Connacht Irish is to be found in <a href="/wiki/Connemara" title="Connemara">Connemara</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Aran_Islands" title="Aran Islands">Aran Islands</a>. Much closer to the larger Connacht Gaeltacht is the dialect spoken in the smaller region on the border between Galway (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Gaillimh</i>) and Mayo (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Maigh Eo</i>).  There are a number of differences between the popular South Connemara form of Irish, the Mid-Connacht/Joyce Country form (on the border between Mayo and Galway) and the Achill and Erris forms in the north of the province.\n</p><p>Features in Connacht Irish differing from the official standard include a preference for verbal nouns ending in <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">-achan</i>, e.g. <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">lagachan</i> instead of <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">lag\xc3\xba</i>, "weakening". The non-standard pronunciation of the <a href="/wiki/Gaeltacht_Cois_Fharraige" title="Gaeltacht Cois Fharraige">Gaeltacht Cois Fharraige</a> area with lengthened vowels and heavily reduced endings gives it a distinct sound. Distinguishing features of Connacht and Ulster dialect include the pronunciation of word-final broad <i>bh</i> and <i>mh</i> as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[w]</span>, rather than as <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[v\xcb\xa0]</span> in Munster. For example, <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">sliabh</i> ("mountain") is pronounced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xca\x83l\xca\xb2i\xc9\x99w]</span> in Connacht and Ulster as opposed to <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xca\x83l\xca\xb2i\xc9\x99\xce\xb2]</span> in the south. In addition Connacht and Ulster speakers tend to include the "we" pronoun rather than use the standard compound form used in Munster, e.g. <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">bh\xc3\xad muid</i> is used for "we were" instead of <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">bh\xc3\xadomar</i>.\n</p><p>As in Munster Irish, some short vowels are lengthened and others diphthongised before -<i>nn</i>, -<i>m</i>, -<i>rr</i>, -<i>rd</i>, -<i>ll</i>, in monosyllabic words and in the stressed syllable of multisyllabic words where the syllable is followed by a consonant. This can be seen in <i>ceann</i> [c\xc9\x91:n] "head", <i>cam</i> [k\xc9\x91:m] "crooked", <i>gearr</i> [g\xca\xb2\xc9\x91:r] "short", <i>ord</i> [ourd] "sledgehammer", <i>gall</i> [g\xc9\x91:l] "foreigner, non-Gael", <i>iontas</i> [i:nt\xc9\x99s] "a wonder, a marvel", etc. The form \'-aibh\', when occurring at the end of words like \'agaibh\', tends to be pronounced as an \'ee\' sound.\n</p><p>In South Connemara, for example, there is a tendency to substitute a "b" sound at the end of words ending in "bh" <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">[\xce\xb2]</span>, such as <i>sibh</i>, <i>libh</i> and <i>d\xc3\xb3ibh</i>, something not found in the rest of Connacht (these words would be pronounced respectively as "shiv," "liv" and "d\xc3\xb3fa" in the other areas). This placing of the B-sound is also present at the end of words ending in vowels, such as <i>acu</i> (pronounced as "acub") and <i>leo</i> (pronounced as "lyohab"). There is also a tendency to omit the "g" sound in words such as <i>agam</i>, <i>agat</i> and <i>againn</i>, a characteristic also of other Connacht dialects. All these pronunciations are distinctively regional.\n</p><p>The pronunciation prevalent in the <a href="/wiki/Joyce_Country" title="Joyce Country">Joyce Country</a> (the area around <a href="/wiki/Lough_Corrib" title="Lough Corrib">Lough Corrib</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lough_Mask" title="Lough Mask">Lough Mask</a>) is quite similar to that of South Connemara, with a similar approach to the words <i>agam</i>, <i>agat</i> and <i>againn</i> and a similar approach to pronunciation of vowels and consonants. But there are noticeable differences in vocabulary, with certain words such as <i>doiligh</i> (difficult) and <i>foscailte</i> being preferred to the more usual <i>deacair</i> and <i>oscailte</i>. Another interesting aspect of this sub-dialect is that almost all vowels at the end of words tend to be pronounced as <i>\xc3\xad</i>: <i>eile</i> (other), <i>cosa</i> (feet) and <i>d\xc3\xa9anta</i> (done) tend to be pronounced as <i>eil\xc3\xad</i>, <i>cosa\xc3\xad</i> and <i>d\xc3\xa9anta\xc3\xad</i> respectively.\n</p><p>The northern Mayo dialect of <a href="/wiki/Erris" title="Erris">Erris</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Iorras</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Achill" class="mw-redirect" title="Achill">Achill</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Acaill</i>) is in grammar and <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a> essentially a Connacht dialect, but shows some similarities to Ulster Irish due to large-scale immigration of dispossessed people following the <a href="/wiki/Plantation_of_Ulster" title="Plantation of Ulster">Plantation of Ulster</a>.  For example, words ending <i>-mh</i> and <i>-bh</i> have a much softer sound, with a tendency to terminate words such as <i>leo</i> and <i>d\xc3\xb3ibh</i> with "f", giving <i>leofa</i> and <i>d\xc3\xb3fa</i> respectively. In addition to a vocabulary typical of other area of Connacht, one also finds Ulster words like <i>amharc</i> (meaning "to look" and pronounced "onk"), <i>nimhneach</i> (painful or sore), <i>druid</i> (close), <i>mothaigh</i> (hear), <i>doiligh</i> (difficult), <i>\xc3\xbar</i> (new), and <i>tig le</i> (to be able to \xe2\x80\x93 i.e. a form similar to <i>f\xc3\xa9idir</i>).\n</p><p>Irish President <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Hyde" title="Douglas Hyde">Douglas Hyde</a> was possibly one of the last speakers of the <a href="/wiki/Roscommon" title="Roscommon">Roscommon</a> dialect of Irish.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ulster">Ulster</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ulster">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Irish" title="Ulster Irish">Ulster Irish</a></div>\n<p>Ulster Irish is the dialect spoken in the Gaeltacht regions of Donegal.  These regions contain all of Ulster\'s communities where Irish has been spoken in an unbroken line back to when the language was the dominant language of Ireland.  The Irish-speaking communities in other parts of Ulster are a result of language revival \xe2\x80\x93 English-speaking families deciding to learn Irish.\n</p><p>Linguistically, the most important of the <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a> dialects today is that which is spoken, with slight differences, in both <a href="/wiki/Gweedore" title="Gweedore">Gweedore</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Gaoth Dobhair</i> = Inlet of Streaming Water) and <a href="/wiki/The_Rosses" title="The Rosses">The Rosses</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">na Rossa</i>).  Natives of Gweedore include singers <a href="/wiki/Enya" title="Enya">Enya</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Eithne</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Moya_Brennan" title="Moya Brennan">Moya Brennan</a> and their siblings in <a href="/wiki/Clannad" title="Clannad">Clannad</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Clann as Dobhar</i> = Family from the Dobhar [a section of Gweedore]) <a href="/wiki/Na_Casaidigh" title="Na Casaidigh">Na Casaidigh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mair%C3%A9ad_N%C3%AD_Mhaonaigh" title="Mair\xc3\xa9ad N\xc3\xad Mhaonaigh">Mair\xc3\xa9ad N\xc3\xad Mhaonaigh</a> from another local band <a href="/wiki/Altan_(band)" title="Altan (band)">Altan</a>.  Natives of The Rosses include the literary brothers <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A9amus_%C3%93_Grianna" title="S\xc3\xa9amus \xc3\x93 Grianna">S\xc3\xa9amus \xc3\x93&#160;Grianna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seosamh_Mac_Grianna" title="Seosamh Mac Grianna">Seosamh Mac&#160;Grianna</a>, locally known as Jim\xc3\xad Fheilim\xc3\xad and Joe Fheilim\xc3\xad.\n</p><p>Ulster Irish sounds quite different to the other two main dialects.  It shares several features with southern dialects of <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manx_language" title="Manx language">Manx</a>, as well as having lots of characteristic words and shades of meanings. However, since the demise of those Irish dialects spoken natively in what is today Northern Ireland, it is probably an exaggeration to see present-day Ulster Irish as an intermediary form between Scottish Gaelic and the southern and western dialects of Irish. Northern Scottish Gaelic has many non-Ulster features in common with Munster Irish.\n</p><p>One noticeable trait of Ulster Irish, Manx Gaelic and Scots Gaelic is the use of the negative particle <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">cha(n)</i> in place of the Munster and Connacht <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">n\xc3\xad</i>. Though southern Donegal Irish tends to use <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">n\xc3\xad</i> more than <i>cha(n)</i>, <i>cha(n)</i> has almost ousted <i>n\xc3\xad</i> in northernmost dialects (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Rosguill" title="Rosguill">Rosguill</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tory_Island" title="Tory Island">Tory Island</a>), though even in these areas <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">n\xc3\xadl</i> "is not" is more common than <i>chan fhuil</i> or <i>cha bhfuil</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another noticeable trait is the pronunciation of the first person singular verb ending <i>-im</i> as <i>-am</i>, also common to Man and Scotland (Munster/Connacht <i>si\xc3\xbalaim</i> "I walk", Ulster <i>si\xc3\xbalam</i>).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leinster">Leinster</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Leinster">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Down to the early 19th century and even later, Irish was spoken in all twelve counties of Leinster. The evidence furnished by placenames, literary sources and recorded speech indicates that there were three dialects spoken in Leinster.  The main dialect was represented by a broad central belt stretching from west Connacht eastwards to the <a href="/wiki/River_Liffey" title="River Liffey">Liffey estuary</a> and southwards to <a href="/wiki/Wexford" title="Wexford">Wexford</a>, though with many local variations.  Two smaller dialects were represented by the Ulster speech of counties Meath and Louth, which extended as far south as the <a href="/wiki/River_Boyne" title="River Boyne">Boyne valley</a>, and a Munster dialect found in Kilkenny and south Laois. \n</p><p>The main dialect had characteristics which survive today only in the Irish of Connacht. It typically placed the stress on the first syllable of a word, and showed a preference (found in placenames) for the pronunciation <i>cr</i> where the standard spelling is <i>cn</i>. The word <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">cnoc</i> (hill) would therefore be pronounced <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">croc</i>. Examples are the placenames Crooksling (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Cnoc Slinne</i>) in County Dublin and Crukeen (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Cnoic\xc3\xadn</i>) in Carlow. East Leinster showed the same diphthongisation or vowel lengthening as in Munster and Connacht Irish in words like <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">poll</i> (hole), <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">cill</i> (monastery), <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">coill</i> (wood), <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">ceann</i> (head), <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">cam</i> (crooked) and <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">dream</i> (crowd). A feature of the dialect was the pronunciation of the vowel <i>ao</i>, which generally became <i>ae</i> in east Leinster (as in Munster), and <i>\xc3\xad</i> in the west (as in Connacht).<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Early evidence regarding colloquial Irish in east Leinster is found in <i>The Fyrst Boke of the Introduction of Knowledge</i> (1547), by the English physician and traveller Andrew Borde.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The illustrative phrases he uses include the following (with regularised Irish spelling in brackets):\n</p>\n<table style="margin-left: 1em;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td>How are you?</td>\n<td><i>Kanys stato?</i></td>\n<td>[<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Canas \'t\xc3\xa1 t\xc3\xba?</i>]\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>I am well, thank you</td>\n<td><i>Tam a goomah gramahagood.</i></td>\n<td>[<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">T\xc3\xa1 m\xc3\xa9 go maith, go raibh maith agat.</i>]\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Syr, can you speak Iryshe?</td>\n<td><i>Sor, woll galow oket?</i></td>\n<td>[<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Sir, \'bhfuil Gaeilig [Gaela\'] agat?</i>]\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Wyfe, gyve me bread!</td>\n<td><i>Benytee, toor haran!</i></td>\n<td>[<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">A bhean an t\xc3\xad, tabhair ar\xc3\xa1n!</i>]\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>How far is it to Waterford?</td>\n<td><i>Gath haad o showh go part laarg?</i>.</td>\n<td>[<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">G\xc3\xa1 fhad as [a] seo go Port L\xc3\xa1irge?</i>]\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>It is one an twenty myle.</td>\n<td><i>Myle hewryht.</i></td>\n<td>[<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">M\xc3\xadle a haon ar fhichid.</i>]\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Whan shal I go to slepe, wyfe?</td>\n<td><i>Gah hon rah moyd holow?</i></td>\n<td>[<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Gathain a racha\xe2\x80\x99 muid a chodladh?</i>]\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Pale">The Pale</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: The Pale">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Pale_According_to_the_Statute_of_1488.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/The_Pale_According_to_the_Statute_of_1488.jpg/190px-The_Pale_According_to_the_Statute_of_1488.jpg" width="190" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/The_Pale_According_to_the_Statute_of_1488.jpg/285px-The_Pale_According_to_the_Statute_of_1488.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/The_Pale_According_to_the_Statute_of_1488.jpg/380px-The_Pale_According_to_the_Statute_of_1488.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2243" data-file-height="2870" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Pale_According_to_the_Statute_of_1488.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Pale \xe2\x80\x93 According to Statute of 1488</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/The_Pale" title="The Pale">The Pale</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">An Ph\xc3\xa1il</i>) was an area around late medieval Dublin under the control of the English government. By the late 15th century it consisted of an area along the coast from <a href="/wiki/Dalkey" title="Dalkey">Dalkey</a>, south of <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>, to the garrison town of <a href="/wiki/Dundalk" title="Dundalk">Dundalk</a>, with an inland boundary encompassing <a href="/wiki/Naas" title="Naas">Naas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leixlip" title="Leixlip">Leixlip</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Earldom_of_Kildare" class="mw-redirect" title="Earldom of Kildare">Earldom of Kildare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trim,_County_Meath" title="Trim, County Meath">Trim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kells,_County_Meath" title="Kells, County Meath">Kells</a> in County Meath to the north. In this area of "Englyshe tunge" English had never actually been a dominant language - and was moreover a relatively late comer; the first colonisers were Normans who spoke Norman French, and before these Norse. The Irish language had always been the language of the bulk of the population. An English official remarked of the Pale in 1515 that "all the common people of the said half counties that obeyeth the King\'s laws, for the most part be of Irish birth, of Irish habit and of Irish language".<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the strengthening of English cultural and political control, language change began to occur, but this did not become clearly evident until the 18th century. Even then, in the decennial period 1771\xe2\x80\x9381, the percentage of Irish speakers in Meath was at least 41%. By 1851 this had fallen to less than 3%.<sup id="cite_ref-Fitzgerald_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzgerald-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="General_decline">General decline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: General decline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>English expanded strongly in Leinster in the 18th century, but Irish speakers were still numerous. In the decennial period 1771\xe2\x80\x9381 certain counties had estimated percentages of Irish speakers as follows (though the estimates are likely to be too low):<sup id="cite_ref-Fitzgerald_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzgerald-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd>Kilkenny 57%</dd>\n<dd>Louth 57%</dd>\n<dd>Longford 22%</dd>\n<dd>Westmeath 17%</dd></dl>\n<p>The language saw its most rapid initial decline in Laois, Wexford, Wicklow, County Dublin and perhaps Kildare.\nThe proportion of Irish-speaking children in Leinster went down as follows: 17% in the 1700s, 11% in the 1800s, 3% in the 1830s and virtually none in the 1860s.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Irish census of 1851 showed that there were still a number of older speakers in County Dublin.<sup id="cite_ref-Fitzgerald_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzgerald-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Sound recordings were made between 1928 and 1931 of some of the last speakers in <a href="/wiki/Omeath" title="Omeath">Omeath</a>, County Louth (now available in digital form).<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The last known traditional native speaker in Omeath, and in Leinster as a whole, was Annie O\'Hanlon (n\xc3\xa9e Dobbin), who died in 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-\xc3\x93_Gr\xc3\xa1da_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-\xc3\x93_Gr\xc3\xa1da-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_aspect">Urban aspect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Urban aspect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Irish was spoken as a community language in Irish towns and cities down to the 19th century. In the 16th and 17th centuries it was widespread even in Dublin and the Pale.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Irish of Dublin, situated as it was between the east Ulster dialect of Meath and Louth to the north and the Leinster-Connacht dialect further south, may have reflected the characteristics of both in phonology and grammar. In County Dublin itself the general rule was to place the stress on the initial vowel of words. With time it appears that the forms of the dative case took over the other case endings in the plural (a tendency found to a lesser extent in other dialects). In a letter written in Dublin in 1691 we find such examples as the following: <i>gn\xc3\xb3thuimh</i> (accusative case, the standard form being <i>gn\xc3\xb3tha\xc3\xad</i>), <i>t\xc3\xadorthuibh</i> (accusative case, the standard form being <i>t\xc3\xadortha</i>) and <i>leithsc\xc3\xa9alaibh</i> (genitive case, the standard form being <i>leithsc\xc3\xa9alta</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>English authorities of the Cromwellian period, aware that Irish was widely spoken in Dublin, arranged for its official use. In 1655 several local dignitaries were ordered to oversee a lecture in Irish to be given in Dublin. In March 1656 a converted Catholic priest, S\xc3\xa9amas Corcy, was appointed to preach in Irish at Bride\xe2\x80\x99s parish every Sunday, and was also ordered to preach at <a href="/wiki/Drogheda" title="Drogheda">Drogheda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Athy" title="Athy">Athy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> In 1657 the English colonists in Dublin presented a petition to the Municipal Council complaining that in Dublin itself "there is Irish commonly and usually spoken".<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is contemporary evidence of the use of Irish in other urban areas at the time. In 1657 it was found necessary to have an Oath of Abjuration (rejecting the authority of the Pope) read in Irish in <a href="/wiki/Cork_(city)" title="Cork (city)">Cork</a> so that people could understand it.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Irish was sufficiently strong in early 18th century Dublin to be the language of a coterie of poets and scribes led by Se\xc3\xa1n and Tadhg \xc3\x93 Neachtain, both poets of note.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Scribal activity in Irish persisted in Dublin right through the 18th century. An outstanding example was Muiris \xc3\x93 Gorm\xc3\xa1in (Maurice Gorman), a prolific producer of manuscripts who advertised his services (in English) in <i>Faulkner\'s Dublin Journal</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In other urban centres the descendants of medieval Anglo-Norman settlers, the so-called <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>, were Irish-speaking or bilingual by the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The English administrator and traveller <a href="/wiki/Fynes_Moryson" title="Fynes Moryson">Fynes Moryson</a>, writing in the last years of the 16th century, said that "the English Irish and the very citizens (excepting those of Dublin where the lord deputy resides) though they could speak English as well as we, yet commonly speak Irish among themselves, and were hardly induced by our familiar conversation to speak English with us".<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The demise of native cultural institutions in the seventeenth century saw the social prestige of Irish diminish, and the gradual Anglicisation of the middle classes followed.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The census of 1851 showed that the towns and cities of Munster still had significant Irish-speaking populations. In 1819 James McQuige, a veteran Methodist lay preacher in Irish, wrote: "In some of the largest southern towns, Cork, Kinsale and even the Protestant town of Bandon, provisions are sold in the markets, and cried in the streets, in Irish".<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Irish speakers constituted over 40% of the population of Cork even in 1851.<sup id="cite_ref-Fitzgerald_57-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzgerald-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 19th century saw a reduction in the number of Dublin\xe2\x80\x99s Irish speakers, in keeping with the trend elsewhere. This continued until the end of the century, when the <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_revival" title="Gaelic revival">Gaelic revival</a> saw the creation of a strong Irish\xe2\x80\x93speaking network, typically united by various branches of the <a href="/wiki/Conradh_na_Gaeilge" title="Conradh na Gaeilge">Conradh na Gaeilge</a>, and accompanied by renewed literary activity.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> By the 1930s Dublin had a lively literary life in Irish.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Urban Irish has been the beneficiary, over the last few decades, of a rapidly expanding independent school system, known generally as <a href="/wiki/Gaelscoil" title="Gaelscoil">Gaelscoileanna</a>. These schools teach entirely through Irish, and there are over thirty in Dublin alone.\n</p><p>It is likely that the number of urban native speakers (i.e. people who were born into Irish-speaking households and educated through Irish) is on the increase. It has been suggested that Ireland\'s towns and cities are acquiring a critical mass of Irish speakers, reflected in the expansion of Irish language media.<sup id="cite_ref-schism_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schism-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Colloquial urban Irish is changing in unforeseen ways, with attention being drawn to the rapid loss of consonantal mutations (which are intrinsic to the language). It is presently uncertain whether the urban Irish of non-native speakers will become a dialect in its own right or grow further apart from native Gaeltacht Irish and become a creole (i.e. a new language).<sup id="cite_ref-schism_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schism-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="An_Caighde.C3.A1n_Oifigi.C3.BAil"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="An_Caighde\xc3\xa1n_Oifigi\xc3\xbail">An Caighde\xc3\xa1n Oifigi\xc3\xbail</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: An Caighde\xc3\xa1n Oifigi\xc3\xbail">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/An_Caighde%C3%A1n_Oifigi%C3%BAil" title="An Caighde\xc3\xa1n Oifigi\xc3\xbail">An Caighde\xc3\xa1n Oifigi\xc3\xbail</a></div>\n<p><i>An Caighde\xc3\xa1n Oifigi\xc3\xbail</i> ("The Official Standard"), often shortened to <i>An Caighde\xc3\xa1n</i>, is the standard for the spelling and grammar of written Irish, developed and used by the Irish government.  Its rules are followed by most schools in Ireland, though schools in and near Irish-speaking regions also use the local dialect.  It was published by the translation department of <a href="/wiki/D%C3%A1il_%C3%89ireann" title="D\xc3\xa1il \xc3\x89ireann">D\xc3\xa1il \xc3\x89ireann</a> in 1953<sup id="cite_ref-blas_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blas-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> and updated in 2012 and 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is no official standard for pronouncing the Irish language.  Certain dictionaries, such as <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Focl\xc3\xb3ir P\xc3\xb3ca</i>, provide a single pronunciation which is followed by most schools in regions which do not have a native dialect.\n</p><p>The differences between dialects are considerable, and have led to recurrent difficulties in defining standard Irish. In recent decades contacts between speakers of different dialects have become more frequent and the differences between the dialects are less noticeable.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Irish_phonology" title="Irish phonology">Irish phonology</a></div>\n<table role="presentation" class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA"><img alt="[]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/30px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png" width="30" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/45px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Parentesi_Quadre.svg/60px-Parentesi_Quadre.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="75" data-file-height="100" /></a></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/International_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="International Phonetic Alphabet">IPA</a> phonetic symbols.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA#Rendering_issues" title="Help:IPA">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a>&#32;instead of <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> characters. For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">Help:IPA</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>In pronunciation, Irish most closely resembles its nearest relatives, Scottish Gaelic and Manx. One notable feature is that consonants (except <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/h/</span>) come in pairs, one "broad" (<a href="/wiki/Velarization" title="Velarization">velarised</a>, pronounced with the back of the tongue pulled back towards the soft palate) and one "slender" (<a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">palatalised</a>, pronounced with the middle of the tongue pushed up towards the hard palate). While broad\xe2\x80\x93slender pairs are not unique to Irish (being found, for example, in <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>), in Irish they have a grammatical function.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">\n\n<caption>Consonant phonemes\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th rowspan="2" colspan="2">\n</th>\n<th colspan="2"><a title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">l\xca\xb2</span>\n</td>\n<td></td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<caption>Vowel phonemes\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th rowspan="2">\n</th>\n<th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;">Front\n</th>\n<th style="text-align:center;">long\n</th>\n<th style="text-align:center;">short\n</th>\n<th style="text-align:center;">short\n</th>\n<th style="text-align:center;">long\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th style="text-align:center;">Close\n</th>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">\xc9\xaa</span></td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">i\xcb\x90</span></td>\n<td></td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">\xca\x8a</span></td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">u\xcb\x90</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th style="text-align:center;">Mid\n</th>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">\xc9\x9b</span></td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">e\xcb\x90</span></td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">\xc9\x99</span></td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">\xc9\x94</span></td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">o\xcb\x90</span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th style="text-align:center;">Open\n</th>\n<td></td>\n<td></td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">a</span></td>\n<td></td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">\xc9\x91\xcb\x90</span>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Diphthongs" class="mw-redirect" title="Diphthongs">Diphthongs</a>: <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">i\xc9\x99, u\xc9\x99, \xc9\x99i, \xc9\x99u</span>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Syntax_and_morphology">Syntax and morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Syntax and morphology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Irish_grammar" title="Irish grammar">Irish grammar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_declension" title="Irish declension">Irish declension</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_conjugation" title="Irish conjugation">Irish conjugation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Irish_syntax" title="Irish syntax">Irish syntax</a></div>\n<p>Irish is a <a href="/wiki/Fusional_language" title="Fusional language">fusional</a>, <a href="/wiki/Verb-subject-object" class="mw-redirect" title="Verb-subject-object">VSO</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nominative-accusative_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Nominative-accusative language">nominative-accusative language</a>. Irish is neither <a href="/wiki/Verb_framing" title="Verb framing">verb</a> nor <a href="/wiki/Verb_framing" title="Verb framing">satellite framed</a>, and makes liberal use of <a href="/wiki/Deixis" title="Deixis">deictic</a> verbs.\n</p><p>Nouns <a href="/wiki/Declension" title="Declension">decline</a> for 3 <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">numbers</a>: <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number#Singular_versus_plural" title="Grammatical number">singular</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dual_(grammatical_number)" title="Dual (grammatical number)">dual</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">plural</a>; 2 <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">genders</a>: masculine, feminine; and 4 <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">cases</a>: ainmneach (<a href="/wiki/Nominative" class="mw-redirect" title="Nominative">nominative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Accusative" class="mw-redirect" title="Accusative">accusative</a>), gairmeach (<a href="/wiki/Vocative" class="mw-redirect" title="Vocative">vocative</a>), ginideach (<a href="/wiki/Genitive" class="mw-redirect" title="Genitive">genitive</a>), and tabharthach (<a href="/wiki/Prepositional_case" title="Prepositional case">prepositional</a>). <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">Adjectives</a> <a href="/wiki/Agreement_(linguistics)" title="Agreement (linguistics)">agree</a> with nouns in <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">number</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">gender</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">case</a>. Adjectives generally follow nouns, though some precede or <a href="/wiki/Prefix" title="Prefix">prefix</a> nouns. <a href="/wiki/Demonstrative" title="Demonstrative">Demonstrative</a> <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">adjectives</a> have <a href="/wiki/Demonstrative#Distal_and_proximal_demonstratives" title="Demonstrative">proximal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demonstrative#Distal_and_proximal_demonstratives" title="Demonstrative">medial</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Demonstrative#Distal_and_proximal_demonstratives" title="Demonstrative">distal</a> forms. The <a href="/wiki/Prepositional_case" title="Prepositional case">prepositional case</a> is called the <a href="/wiki/Dative" class="mw-redirect" title="Dative">dative</a> by convention.\n</p><p>Verbs <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjugation" title="Grammatical conjugation">conjugate</a> for 3 <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">tenses</a>: <a href="/wiki/Past_tense" title="Past tense">past</a>, <a href="/wiki/Present_tense" title="Present tense">present</a>, <a href="/wiki/Future_tense" title="Future tense">future</a>; 2 <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">aspects</a>: <a href="/wiki/Uses_of_English_verb_forms#Simple" title="Uses of English verb forms">simple</a>, <a href="/wiki/Habitual_aspect" title="Habitual aspect">habitual</a>; 2 numbers: <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number#Singular_versus_plural" title="Grammatical number">singular</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">plural</a>; 4 <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">moods</a>: <a href="/wiki/Indicative_mood" class="mw-redirect" title="Indicative mood">indicative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subjunctive_mood" title="Subjunctive mood">subjunctive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conditional_mood" title="Conditional mood">conditional</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imperative_mood" title="Imperative mood">imperative</a>; <a href="/wiki/Connacht_Irish#Verbs" title="Connacht Irish">relative forms</a>; and in some verbs, <a href="/wiki/Dependent_and_independent_verb_forms" title="Dependent and independent verb forms">independent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dependent_and_independent_verb_forms" title="Dependent and independent verb forms">dependent</a> forms. Verbs <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjugation" title="Grammatical conjugation">conjugate</a> for 3 <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">persons</a> and an impersonal form in which no <a href="/wiki/Agent_(grammar)" title="Agent (grammar)">agent</a> can be determined. There are two verbs for "to be", one for <a href="/wiki/Essence" title="Essence">inherent qualities</a>, and one for <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory#Aristotle" title="Substance theory">transient qualities</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Passive_voice" title="Passive voice">passive voice</a> and many other forms are <a href="/wiki/Periphrasis" title="Periphrasis">periphrastic</a>. There are a number of preverbal <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_particle" title="Grammatical particle">particles</a> marking the <a href="/wiki/Affirmative_and_negative" class="mw-redirect" title="Affirmative and negative">negative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interrogative" title="Interrogative">interrogative</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subjunctive_mood" title="Subjunctive mood">subjunctive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Relative_clause" title="Relative clause">relative clauses</a>, etc. There is a <a href="/wiki/Verbal_noun" title="Verbal noun">verbal noun</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Verbal_adjective" class="mw-redirect" title="Verbal adjective">verbal adjective</a>. Verb forms are highly <a href="/wiki/Inflection#Regular_and_irregular_inflection" title="Inflection">regular</a>, many grammars recognise only <a href="/wiki/Irish_conjugation#Irregular_verbs" title="Irish conjugation">11 irregular verbs</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Preposition_and_postposition" title="Preposition and postposition">Prepositions</a> <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflect</a> for <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">person</a> and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">number</a>. Different prepositions <a href="/wiki/Government_(linguistics)" title="Government (linguistics)">govern</a> different <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">cases</a>. Some prepositions <a href="/wiki/Government_(linguistics)" title="Government (linguistics)">govern</a> different cases depending on intended <a href="/wiki/Semantics" title="Semantics">semantics</a>.  The word <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">ag</i> (at), becomes <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">agam</i> (at me) in the first person singular. When used with the verb <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">b\xc3\xad</i> (to be), <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">ag</i> indicates <a href="/wiki/Possession_(linguistics)" title="Possession (linguistics)">possession</a>. Irish shares this attribute with Russian.\n</p>\n<ul><li><span lang="ga" title="Irish language text"><i>T\xc3\xa1 leabhar</i> agam.</span> "I have a book." (Literally, "there is a book at (on) me," cf. Russian "\xd0\xa3 \xd0\xbc\xd0\xb5\xd0\xbd\xd1\x8f \xd0\xb5\xd1\x81\xd1\x82\xd1\x8c \xd0\xba\xd0\xbd\xd0\xb8\xd0\xb3\xd0\xb0")</li>\n<li><span lang="ga" title="Irish language text"><i>T\xc3\xa1 leabhar</i> agat.</span> "You have a book."</li>\n<li><span lang="ga" title="Irish language text"><i>T\xc3\xa1 leabhar</i> aige.</span> "He has a book."</li>\n<li><span lang="ga" title="Irish language text"><i>T\xc3\xa1 leabhar</i> aici.</span> "She has a book."</li>\n<li><span lang="ga" title="Irish language text"><i>T\xc3\xa1 leabhar</i> againn.</span> "We have a book."</li>\n<li><span lang="ga" title="Irish language text"><i>T\xc3\xa1 leabhar</i> agaibh.</span> "Ye have a book."</li>\n<li><span lang="ga" title="Irish language text"><i>T\xc3\xa1 leabhar</i> acu.</span> "They have a book."</li></ul>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Numerals_(linguistics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Numerals (linguistics)">Numerals</a> have 4 forms: abstract, impersonal, personal, and ordinal.\n</p>\n<ul><li><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">"a d\xc3\xb3"</i> Two.</li>\n<li><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">"dh\xc3\xa1 leabhar"</i> Two books.</li>\n<li><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">"beirt"</i> Two people.</li>\n<li><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">"dara"</i> Second.</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_mutations">Initial mutations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Initial mutations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Irish_initial_mutations" title="Irish initial mutations">Irish initial mutations</a></div>\n<p>In Irish, there are two classes of initial <a href="/wiki/Consonant_mutation" title="Consonant mutation">consonant mutations</a>, which express grammatical relationship and meaning in verbs, nouns and adjectives:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Lenition" title="Lenition">Lenition</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">s\xc3\xa9imhi\xc3\xba</i>) describes the change of stops into fricatives. Indicated in <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_type" title="Gaelic type">Gaelic script</a> by a <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">s\xc3\xad buailte</i> (a dot) written above the consonant, it is shown in <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a> by adding a <i>h</i>.\n<ul><li><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">caith!</i> "throw!" \xe2\x80\x93 <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">chaith m\xc3\xa9</i> "I threw" (lenition as a past-tense marker, caused by the particle <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">do</i>, now generally omitted)</li>\n<li><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">g\xc3\xa1</i> "requirement" \xe2\x80\x93 <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">easpa an gh\xc3\xa1</i> "lack of the requirement" (lenition marking the genitive case of a masculine noun)</li>\n<li><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Se\xc3\xa1n</i> "John" \xe2\x80\x93 <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">a She\xc3\xa1in!</i> "John!" (lenition as part of the vocative case, the vocative lenition being triggered by <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">a</i>, the vocative marker before <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">She\xc3\xa1in</i>)</li></ul></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Irish_initial_mutations#Eclipsis" title="Irish initial mutations">Eclipsis</a> (<i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">ur\xc3\xba</i>) covers the voicing of voiceless stops, and <a href="/wiki/Nasalisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Nasalisation">nasalisation</a> of voiced stops.\n<ul><li><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">athair</i> "father" \xe2\x80\x93 <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">\xc3\xa1r n-Athair</i> "our Father"</li>\n<li><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">t\xc3\xbas</i> "start", <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">ar dt\xc3\xbas</i> "at the start"</li>\n<li><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Gaillimh</i> "Galway" \xe2\x80\x93 <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">i nGaillimh</i> "in Galway"</li></ul></li></ul>\n<p>Mutations are often the only way to distinguish grammatical forms. For example, the only non-contextual way to distinguish <a href="/wiki/Pronoun#Possessive" title="Pronoun">possessive pronouns</a> "her," "his" and "their", is through initial mutations since all meanings are represented by the same word <i>a</i>.\n</p>\n<ul><li>their shoe \xe2\x80\x93 <i>a mbr\xc3\xb3g</i> (eclipsis)</li>\n<li>his shoe \xe2\x80\x93 <i>a bhr\xc3\xb3g</i> (lenition)</li>\n<li>her shoe \xe2\x80\x93 <i>a br\xc3\xb3g</i> (unchanged)</li></ul>\n<p>Due to <a href="/wiki/Irish_initial_mutations" title="Irish initial mutations">initial mutation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prefix" title="Prefix">prefixes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">clitics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suffix" title="Suffix">suffixes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">root</a> <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">inflection</a>, ending <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elision" title="Elision">elision</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sandhi" title="Sandhi">sandhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epinthesis" class="mw-redirect" title="Epinthesis">epinthesis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Assimilation_(phonology)" title="Assimilation (phonology)">assimilation</a>; the beginning, core, and end of words can each change radically and even simultaneously depending on context.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">Orthography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Orthography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Irish_orthography" title="Irish orthography">Irish orthography</a></div>\n<p>Modern Irish traditionally used the <a href="/wiki/ISO_basic_Latin_alphabet" title="ISO basic Latin alphabet">ISO basic Latin alphabet</a> without the letters j, k, q, w, x, y and z, but with the addition of one <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">diacritic</a> sign, the <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">acute accent</a> (<i>\xc3\xa1 \xc3\xa9 \xc3\xad \xc3\xb3 \xc3\xba</i>), known in Irish as the <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">s\xc3\xadneadh fada</i> ("long mark"; plural: <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">s\xc3\xadnte fada</i>). However, some gaelicised words use those letters: for instance, \'Jeep\' is written as \'J\xc3\xadp\' (the letter v has been naturalised into the language, although it is not part of the traditional alphabet, and has the same pronunciation as "bh"). In idiomatic English usage, this diacritic is frequently referred to simply as the <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">fada</i>, where the adjective is used as a noun. The <i>fada</i> serves to lengthen the sound of the vowels and in some cases also changes their quality. For example, in Munster Irish (Kerry), <i>a</i> is <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/a/</span> or <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x91/</span> and <i>\xc3\xa1</i> is <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x91\xcb\x90/</span> in "father" but in Ulster Irish (Donegal), <i>\xc3\xa1</i> tends to be <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc3\xa6\xcb\x90/</span>.\n</p><p>Traditional orthography had an additional diacritic&#160;&#8211;&#32;a <a href="/wiki/Dot_(diacritic)" title="Dot (diacritic)">dot</a> over some consonants to indicate <a href="/wiki/Lenition" title="Lenition">lenition</a>. In modern Irish, the letter h suffixed to a consonant indicates that the consonant is lenited. Thus, for example, \'Gaela\xc4\x8b\' has become \'Gaelach\'.\n</p><p>Around the time of the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, S\xc3\xa9amas Dalt\xc3\xban, in charge of <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Rann\xc3\xb3g an Aistri\xc3\xbach\xc3\xa1in</i> (the official translations department of the Irish government), issued his own guidelines about how to standardise Irish spelling and grammar. This de facto standard was subsequently approved by the State and called the Official Standard or <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Caighde\xc3\xa1n Oifigi\xc3\xbail</i>. It simplified and standardised the orthography. Many words had silent letters removed and vowel combination brought closer to the spoken language. Where multiple versions existed in different dialects for the same word, one or more were selected.\n</p><p>Examples:\n</p>\n<ul><li><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Gaedhealg / Gaedhilg(e) / Gaedhealaing / Gaeilic / Gaelainn / Gaoidhealg / Gaolainn</i> \xe2\x86\x92  <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Gaeilge</i>, "Irish language"</li>\n<li><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">Lughbhaidh</i>  \xe2\x86\x92  <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">L\xc3\xba</i>, "Louth" (see <a href="/wiki/County_Louth_Historic_Names" title="County Louth Historic Names">County Louth Historic Names</a>)</li>\n<li><i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">biadh</i>  \xe2\x86\x92  <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">bia</i>, "food"</li></ul>\n<p>The standard spelling does not necessarily reflect the pronunciation used in particular dialects. For example, in standard Irish, <i>bia</i>, "food", has the genitive <i>bia</i>. In Munster Irish, however, the genitive is pronounced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/b\xca\xb2i\xcb\x90\xc9\x9f/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> For this reason, the spelling <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">biadh</i> is still used by the speakers of some dialects, in particular those that show a meaningful and audible difference between <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">biadh</i> (nominative case) and <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">b\xc3\xaddh</i> (genitive case) "of food, food\'s". In Munster the latter spelling regularly produces the pronunciation <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/b\xca\xb2i\xcb\x90\xc9\x9f/</span> because final <i>-idh, -igh</i> regularly delenites to <i>-ig</i> in Munster pronunciation. Another example would be the word <i>crua</i>, meaning "hard". This pronounced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/kru\xc9\x99\xc9\x9f/</span><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> in Munster, in line with the pre-Caighde\xc3\xa1n spelling, <i>cruaidh</i>. In Munster, <i>ao</i> is pronounced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/e\xcb\x90/</span> and <i>aoi</i> pronounced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/i\xcb\x90/</span>,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> but the new spellings of <i>saoghal</i>, "life, world", genitive: <i>saoghail</i>, have become <i>saol</i>, genitive <i>saoil</i>. This produces irregularities in the match-up between the spelling and pronunciation in Munster, because the word is pronounced <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/s\xcb\xa0e\xcb\x90l\xcc\xaa\xcb\xa0/</span>, genitive <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/s\xcb\xa0e\xcb\x90l\xca\xb2/</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Dot_(diacritic)" title="Dot (diacritic)">dot-above diacritic</a>, called a <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">ponc s\xc3\xa9imhithe</i> or <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">s\xc3\xad buailte</i> (often shortened to <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">buailte</i>), derives from the <i>punctum delens</i> used in medieval manuscripts to indicate deletion, similar to crossing out unwanted words in handwriting today. From this usage it was used to indicate the <a href="/wiki/Lenition" title="Lenition">lenition</a> of <i>s</i> (from <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/s/</span> to <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/h/</span>) and <i>f</i> (from <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/f/</span> to <a href="/wiki/Null_morpheme" title="Null morpheme">zero</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Old_Irish" title="Old Irish">Old Irish</a> texts.\n</p><p>Lenition of <i>c</i>, <i>p</i>, and <i>t</i> was indicated by placing the letter <i>h</i> after the affected consonant; lenition of other sounds was left unmarked. Later both methods were extended to be indicators of lenition of any sound except <i>l</i> and <i>n</i>, and two competing systems were used: lenition could be marked by a <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">buailte</i> or by a postposed <i>h</i>. Eventually, use of the <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">buailte</i> predominated when texts were written using Gaelic letters, while the <i>h</i> predominated when writing using Roman letters.\n</p><p>Today, <a href="/wiki/Gaelic_type" title="Gaelic type">Gaelic type</a> and the <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">buailte</i> are rarely used except where a "traditional" style is required, e.g. the motto on the <a href="/wiki/University_College_Dublin" title="University College Dublin">University College Dublin</a> coat of arms<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> or the symbol of the Irish Defence Forces, The <a href="/wiki/Irish_Defence_Forces_cap_badge" title="Irish Defence Forces cap badge">Irish Defence Forces cap badge</a> <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">(\xc3\x93glai\xc4\xa1 na h-\xc3\x89ireann)</i>. Letters with the <i lang="ga" title="Irish language text">buailte</i> are available in <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a> and <a href="/wiki/ISO_8859-14" class="mw-redirect" title="ISO 8859-14">Latin-8</a> <a href="/wiki/Character_set" class="mw-redirect" title="Character set">character sets</a> (see Latin Extended Additional chart).<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Irish_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; -webkit-column-width: 20em; column-width: 20em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/B%C3%A9arlachas" title="B\xc3\xa9arlachas">B\xc3\xa9arlachas</a>, Anglicisms in Irish</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Bunt%C3%BAs_Cainte" title="Bunt\xc3\xbas Cainte">Bunt\xc3\xbas Cainte</a>, a course in basic spoken Irish</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Cumann_Gaelach" title="Cumann Gaelach">Cumann Gaelach</a>, Irish language Society</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Dictionary_of_the_Irish_Language" title="Dictionary of the Irish Language">Dictionary of the Irish Language</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_Scottish_Gaelic_and_Irish" title="Comparison of Scottish Gaelic and Irish">Comparison of Scottish Gaelic and Irish</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Goidelic_substrate_hypothesis" title="Goidelic substrate hypothesis">Goidelic substrate hypothesis</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Hiberno-Latin" title="Hiberno-Latin">Hiberno-Latin</a>, a variety of <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a> used in Irish monasteries. It included Greek, Hebrew and Celtic neologisms.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Irish_name" title="Irish name">Irish name</a> and <a href="/wiki/Place_names_in_Ireland" title="Place names in Ireland">Place
